			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Sustainability</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability_governance&amp;redirect=no" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainability governance">Sustainability governance</a>)</span></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A view of the Earth from space." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg/220px-North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg/330px-North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg/440px-North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg 2x" data-file-width="8000" data-file-height="8000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:North_America_from_low_orbiting_satellite_Suomi_NPP.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Achieving sustainability will enable the Earth to continue supporting human life.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecology</a>, <b>sustainability</b> (from <i>sustain</i> and <i>ability</i>) is the property of <a href="/wiki/Biological_system" title="Biological system">biological systems</a> to remain <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">diverse</a> and productive <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/indefinite#Adjective" class="extiw" title="wikt:indefinite">indefinitely</a>. Long-lived and healthy <a href="/wiki/Wetlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a> and <a href="/wiki/Forests" class="mw-redirect" title="Forests">forests</a> are examples of sustainable biological systems. In more general terms, sustainability is the endurance of systems and processes. The <a href="/wiki/Organizing_principle" title="Organizing principle">organizing principle</a> for sustainability is <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>, which includes the four interconnected domains: ecology, economics, politics and culture.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_science" title="Sustainability science">Sustainability science</a> is the study of sustainable development and environmental science.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sustainability can also be defined as a socio-ecological process characterized by the pursuit of a common ideal.<sup id="cite_ref-Dr._Joel_Diemer_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dr._Joel_Diemer-3">[3]</a></sup> An ideal is by definition unattainable in a given time/space but endlessly approachable and it is this endless pursuit that forms a sustainable system in the process (ibid). Healthy ecosystems and <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environments</a> are necessary to the survival of humans and other <a href="/wiki/Organisms" class="mw-redirect" title="Organisms">organisms</a>. Ways of reducing negative human impact are <a href="/wiki/Chemical_engineering" title="Chemical engineering">environmentally-friendly chemical engineering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_resources_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental resources management">environmental resources management</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_protection" title="Environmental protection">environmental protection</a>. Information is gained from <a href="/wiki/Green_chemistry" title="Green chemistry">green chemistry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">earth science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a>. <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">Ecological economics</a> studies the fields of academic research that aim to address human economies and natural ecosystems.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg/220px-Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg/330px-Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg/440px-Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3782" data-file-height="2592" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Inside_the_Batad_rice_terraces.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Batad <a href="/wiki/Rice_terrace" class="mw-redirect" title="Rice terrace">rice terraces</a>, The Philippines&#160;—UNESCO World Heritage site</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moving towards sustainability is also a social challenge that entails <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international</a> and national <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> and <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">transport</a>, local and individual <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumerism</a>. Ways of living more sustainably can take many forms from reorganizing living conditions (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Ecovillages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecovillages">ecovillages</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eco-municipalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Eco-municipalities">eco-municipalities</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable cities">sustainable cities</a>), reappraising economic sectors (<a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">permaculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_building" title="Green building">green building</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>), or work practices (<a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a>), using science to develop new technologies (<a href="/wiki/Green_technologies" class="mw-redirect" title="Green technologies">green technologies</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> and sustainable <a href="/wiki/Generation_IV_reactor" title="Generation IV reactor">fission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fusion_power" title="Fusion power">fusion power</a>), or designing systems in a flexible and reversible manner,<sup id="cite_ref-tandfonline.com_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline.com-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhang_2012_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang_2012-5">[5]</a></sup> and adjusting individual <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyles</a> that conserve natural resources.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>
<p>"The term 'sustainability' should be viewed as humanity's target goal of human-ecosystem equilibrium (homeostasis), while 'sustainable development' refers to the holistic approach and temporal processes that lead us to the end point of sustainability." (305)<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Despite the increased popularity of the use of the term "sustainability", the possibility that human societies will achieve environmental sustainability has been, and continues to be, questioned—in light of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, <a href="/wiki/Overconsumption" title="Overconsumption">overconsumption</a>, population growth and societies' pursuit of unlimited <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> in a <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">Sustainable development</a></div>
<p>The name sustainability is derived from the <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> <i>sustinere</i> (<i>tenere</i>, to hold; <i>sub</i>, up). <i>Sustain</i> can mean “maintain", "support", or "endure”.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup> Since the 1980s <i>sustainability</i> has been used more in the sense of human sustainability on planet Earth and this has resulted in the most widely quoted definition of sustainability as a part of the concept <i><a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a></i>, that of the <a href="/wiki/Brundtland_Commission" title="Brundtland Commission">Brundtland Commission</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> on March 20, 1987: “sustainable development is development that meets the needs of the present without compromising the ability of future generations to meet their own needs.”<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Components">Components</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Components">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Three_pillars_of_sustainability">Three pillars of sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Three pillars of sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Nested_sustainability-v2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nested_sustainability-v2.svg/250px-Nested_sustainability-v2.svg.png" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nested_sustainability-v2.svg/375px-Nested_sustainability-v2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nested_sustainability-v2.svg/500px-Nested_sustainability-v2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>

<tr style="font-size: 87%; line-height:134%">
<td style="border: 0px;"><a href="/wiki/Venn_diagram" title="Venn diagram">Venn diagram</a> of sustainable development:<br />
at the confluence of three constituent parts<sup id="cite_ref-Adams2006_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Adams2006-15">[15]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/2005_World_Summit" title="2005 World Summit">2005 World Summit on Social Development</a> identified sustainable development goals, such as economic development, social development and environmental protection.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> This view has been expressed as an illustration using three overlapping ellipses indicating that the three pillars of sustainability are not mutually exclusive and can be mutually reinforcing.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> In fact, the three pillars are interdependent, and in the long run none can exist without the others.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The three pillars have served as a common ground for numerous <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_standards_and_certification" title="Sustainability standards and certification">sustainability standards and certification</a> systems in recent years, in particular in the food industry.<sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> Standards which today explicitly refer to the triple bottom line include <a href="/wiki/Rainforest_Alliance" title="Rainforest Alliance">Rainforest Alliance</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade">Fairtrade</a> and <a href="/wiki/UTZ_Certified" title="UTZ Certified">UTZ Certified</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Some sustainability experts and practitioners have illustrated four pillars of sustainability, or a quadruple bottom line. One such pillar is future generations, which emphasizes the long-term thinking associated with sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> There is also an opinion that considers resource use and financial sustainability as two additional pillars of sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sustainable development consists of balancing local and global efforts to meet basic human needs without destroying or degrading the natural environment.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> The question then becomes how to represent the relationship between those needs and the environment.</p>
<p>A study from 2005 pointed out that <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">environmental justice</a> is as important as sustainable development.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Ecological economist <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">Herman Daly</a> asked, "what use is a sawmill without a forest?"<sup id="cite_ref-Daly_.26_Cobb_1989_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daly_.26_Cobb_1989-29">[29]</a></sup> From this perspective, the economy is a subsystem of human society, which is itself a subsystem of the biosphere, and a gain in one sector is a loss from another.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> This perspective led to the nested circles figure of 'economics' inside 'society' inside the 'environment'.</p>
<p>The simple definition that sustainability is something that improves "the <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of human life</a> while living within the carrying capacity of supporting eco-systems",<sup id="cite_ref-caring_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caring-31">[31]</a></sup> though vague, conveys the idea of sustainability having quantifiable limits. But sustainability is also a call to action, a task in progress or “journey” and therefore a political process, so some definitions set out common goals and values.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Earth_Charter" title="Earth Charter">Earth Charter</a><sup id="cite_ref-EarthCharter_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarthCharter-33">[33]</a></sup> speaks of “a sustainable global society founded on respect for nature, universal human rights, economic justice, and a culture of peace.” This suggested a more complex figure of sustainability, which included the importance of the domain of 'politics'.</p>
<p>More than that, sustainability implies responsible and proactive decision-making and innovation that minimizes negative impact and maintains balance between ecological resilience, economic prosperity, political justice and cultural vibrancy to ensure a desirable planet for all species now and in the future.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Specific types of sustainability include, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a> or <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Understanding sustainable development is important but without clear targets an unfocused term like "liberty" or "justice".<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> It has also been described as a "dialogue of values that challenge the sociology of development".<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Circles_of_sustainability">Circles of sustainability</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Circles of sustainability">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_Profile,_Level_1,_2012.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg/220px-Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg" width="220" height="237" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg/330px-Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg/440px-Sao_Paulo_Profile%2C_Level_1%2C_2012.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1074" data-file-height="1156" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sao_Paulo_Profile,_Level_1,_2012.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Urban sustainability analysis of the greater urban area of the city of São Paulo using the ‘Circles of Sustainability' method of the UN and Metropolis Association.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>While the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Millennium_Declaration" title="United Nations Millennium Declaration">United Nations Millennium Declaration</a> identified principles and treaties on sustainable development, including economic development, <a href="/wiki/Social_sustainability" title="Social sustainability">social development</a> and environmental protection it continued using three domains: economics, environment and social sustainability. More recently, using a systematic domain model that responds to the debates over the last decade, the <a href="/wiki/Circles_of_Sustainability" title="Circles of Sustainability">Circles of Sustainability</a> approach distinguished four domains of economic, ecological, political and cultural sustainability. This in accord with the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Agenda_21" title="Agenda 21">Agenda 21</a>, which specifies <b>culture</b> as the fourth domain of sustainable development.<sup id="cite_ref-agenda21culture.net_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-agenda21culture.net-39">[39]</a></sup> The model is now being used by organizations such as the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Global_Compact" title="United Nations Global Compact">United Nations</a> Cities Programme.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> and Metropolis<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Seven_modalities">Seven modalities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Seven modalities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Another model suggests humans attempt to achieve all of their needs and aspirations via seven modalities: economy, community, occupational groups, government, environment, culture, and physiology.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> From the global to the individual human scale, each of the seven modalities can be viewed across seven hierarchical levels. Human sustainability can be achieved by attaining sustainability in all levels of the seven modalities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Shaping_the_future">Shaping the future</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Shaping the future">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Integral elements of sustainability are research and innovation activities. A telling example is the <a href="/wiki/European_environmental_research_and_innovation_policy" title="European environmental research and innovation policy">European environmental research and innovation policy</a>. It aims at defining and implementing a transformative agenda to greening the economy and the society as a whole so to make them sustainable. Research and innovation in Europe are financially supported by the programme <a href="/wiki/Horizon_2020" title="Horizon 2020">Horizon 2020</a>, which is also open to participation worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Encouraging good farming practices ensures farmers fully benefit from the environment and at the same time conserving it for future generations.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Resiliency">Resiliency</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Resiliency">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/resilience" class="extiw" title="wikt:resilience">Resiliency</a> in ecology is the capacity of an ecosystem to absorb disturbance and still retain its basic structure and viability. Resilience-thinking evolved from the need to manage interactions between human-constructed systems and natural ecosystems in a sustainable way despite the fact that to <a href="/wiki/Policymaker" class="mw-redirect" title="Policymaker">policymakers</a> a definition remains elusive. Resilience-thinking addresses how much planetary ecological systems can withstand assault from human disturbances and still deliver the services current and future generations need from them. It is also concerned with commitment from <a href="/wiki/Geopolitical" class="mw-redirect" title="Geopolitical">geopolitical</a> policymakers to promote and manage essential planetary ecological resources in order to promote resilience and achieve sustainability of these essential resources for benefit of future generations of life?<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The resiliency of an ecosystem, and thereby, its sustainability, can be reasonably measured at <a href="/wiki/Juncture" title="Juncture">junctures</a> or events where the combination of <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">naturally</a> occurring <a href="/wiki/Regenerative_(design)" class="mw-redirect" title="Regenerative (design)">regenerative</a> forces (<a href="/wiki/Solar_energy" title="Solar energy">solar energy</a>, water, soil, <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, vegetation, and <a href="/wiki/Biomass" title="Biomass">biomass</a>) interact with the energy released into the ecosystem from disturbances.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup></p>
<p>A practical view of sustainability is <a href="/wiki/Closed_system" title="Closed system">closed systems</a> that maintain processes of <a href="/wiki/Productivity" title="Productivity">productivity</a> indefinitely by replacing resources used by actions of people with resources of equal or greater value by those same people without degrading or endangering natural biotic systems.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> In this way, sustainability can be concretely measured in human projects if there is a transparent accounting of the resources put back into the ecosystem to replace those displaced. In nature, the accounting occurs naturally through a process of <a href="/wiki/Adaptation" title="Adaptation">adaptation</a> as an ecosystem returns to <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/viability" class="extiw" title="wikt:viability">viability</a> from an external disturbance. The adaptation is a multi-stage process that begins with the disturbance event (earthquake, volcanic eruption, hurricane, tornado, flood, or thunderstorm), followed by <a href="/wiki/Dissipation" title="Dissipation">absorption</a>, <a href="/wiki/Utilization" title="Utilization">utilization</a>, or <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/rejection" class="extiw" title="wikt:rejection">deflection</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a> or energies that the external forces created.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In analysing systems such as urban and national parks, dams, farms and gardens, theme parks, open-pit mines, water catchments, one way to look at the relationship between sustainability and resiliency is to view the former with a long-term vision and resiliency as the capacity of human engineers to respond to immediate environmental events.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_sustainability" title="History of sustainability">History of sustainability</a></div>
<p>The history of sustainability traces human-dominated <a href="/wiki/Ecology" title="Ecology">ecological</a> systems from the earliest <a href="/wiki/Civilization" title="Civilization">civilizations</a> to the present time.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> This history is characterized by the increased regional success of a particular <a href="/wiki/Society" title="Society">society</a>, followed by crises that were either resolved, producing sustainability, or not, leading to decline.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>In early human history, the use of fire and desire for specific foods may have altered the natural composition of plant and animal communities.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Between 8,000 and 10,000 years ago, <a href="/wiki/Agrarian_community" class="mw-redirect" title="Agrarian community">agrarian communities</a> emerged which depended largely on their <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">environment</a> and the creation of a "structure of permanence."<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Western <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> of the 18th to 19th centuries tapped into the vast growth potential of the energy in <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>. <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">Coal</a> was used to power ever more efficient engines and later to generate electricity. Modern <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a> systems and advances in medicine protected large populations from disease.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> In the mid-20th century, a gathering <a href="/wiki/Environmental_movement" title="Environmental movement">environmental movement</a> pointed out that there were environmental costs associated with the many material benefits that were now being enjoyed. In the late 20th century, environmental problems became global in scale.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LPR_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LPR-56">[56]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> The 1973 and 1979 <a href="/wiki/Energy_crisis" title="Energy crisis">energy crises</a> demonstrated the extent to which the global community had become dependent on non-renewable energy resources.</p>
<p>In the 21st century, there is increasing global awareness of the threat posed by the human <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_effect" title="Greenhouse effect">greenhouse effect</a>, produced largely by forest clearing and the burning of fossil fuels.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Principles_and_concepts">Principles and concepts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Principles and concepts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The philosophical and analytic framework of sustainability draws on and connects with many different disciplines and fields; in recent years an area that has come to be called <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_science" title="Sustainability science">sustainability science</a> has emerged.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Scale_and_context">Scale and context</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Scale and context">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sustainability is studied and managed over many scales (levels or frames of reference) of time and space and in many contexts of environmental, social and economic organization. The focus ranges from the total <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> (sustainability) of planet Earth to the sustainability of economic sectors, ecosystems, countries, municipalities, neighbourhoods, home gardens, individual lives, individual goods and services<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (January 2012)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>, occupations, lifestyles, behaviour patterns and so on. In short, it can entail the full compass of biological and human activity or any part of it.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> As Daniel Botkin, author and environmentalist, has stated: "We see a landscape that is always in flux, changing over many scales of time and space."<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>The sheer size and complexity of the planetary ecosystem has proved problematic for the design of practical measures to reach global sustainability. To shed light on the big picture, explorer and sustainability campaigner <a href="/wiki/Jason_Lewis_(adventurer)" title="Jason Lewis (adventurer)">Jason Lewis</a> has drawn parallels to other, more tangible <a href="/wiki/Closed_systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Closed systems">closed systems</a>. For example, he likens human existence on Earth&#160;— isolated as the planet is in space, whereby people cannot be evacuated to relieve population pressure and resources cannot be imported to prevent <a href="/w/index.php?title=Accelerated_depletion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Accelerated depletion (page does not exist)">accelerated depletion</a> of resources&#160;— to life at sea on a small boat isolated by water.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> In both cases, he argues, exercising the <a href="/wiki/Precautionary_principle" title="Precautionary principle">precautionary principle</a> is a key factor in survival.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consumption">Consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A major driver of human impact on Earth systems is the destruction of <a href="/wiki/Biophysical" class="mw-redirect" title="Biophysical">biophysical</a> <a href="/wiki/Resources" class="mw-redirect" title="Resources">resources</a>, and especially, the Earth's ecosystems. The environmental impact of a community or of humankind as a whole depends both on population and impact per person, which in turn depends in complex ways on what resources are being used, whether or not those resources are renewable, and the scale of the human activity relative to the carrying capacity of the ecosystems involved. Careful resource management can be applied at many scales, from economic sectors like agriculture, manufacturing and industry, to work organizations, the consumption patterns of households and individuals and to the resource demands of individual goods and services.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brower_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brower-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the initial attempts to express human impact mathematically was developed in the 1970s and is called the <a href="/wiki/I_PAT" class="mw-redirect" title="I PAT">I PAT</a> formula. This formulation attempts to explain human consumption in terms of three components: <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">population</a> numbers, levels of consumption (which it terms "affluence", although the usage is different), and impact per unit of resource use (which is termed "technology", because this impact depends on the <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> used). The equation is expressed:</p>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>I = P × A × T</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>
<dl>
<dd>Where: I = Environmental impact, P = Population, A = Affluence, T = Technology<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrlich.26Holden_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrlich.26Holden-68">[68]</a></sup></dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
</dd>
</dl>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Measurement">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_measurement" title="Sustainability measurement">Sustainability measurement</a></div>
<p>Sustainability measurement is a term that denotes the measurements used as the quantitative basis for the informed management of sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> The metrics used for the measurement of sustainability (involving the sustainability of environmental, social and economic domains, both individually and in various combinations) are evolving: they include <a href="/wiki/Ecological_indicator" title="Ecological indicator">indicators</a>, benchmarks, audits, <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_standards_and_certification" title="Sustainability standards and certification">sustainability standards and certification</a> systems like <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade">Fairtrade</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_certification" title="Organic certification">Organic</a>, indexes and accounting, as well as assessment, appraisal<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and other reporting systems. They are applied over a wide range of spatial and temporal scales.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some of the best known and most widely used sustainability measures include corporate <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_reporting" title="Sustainability reporting">sustainability reporting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Triple_bottom_line" title="Triple bottom line">Triple Bottom Line accounting</a>, World Sustainability Society, <a href="/wiki/Circles_of_Sustainability" title="Circles of Sustainability">Circles of Sustainability</a>, and estimates of the quality of sustainability governance for individual countries using the <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Sustainability_Index" title="Environmental Sustainability Index">Environmental Sustainability Index</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Performance_Index" title="Environmental Performance Index">Environmental Performance Index</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Population">Population</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Population">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_population_control" class="mw-redirect" title="Human population control">Human population control</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Population_curve.svg" class="image"><img alt="Graph showing human population growth from 10,000 BC&#160;– 2000 AD, illustrating current exponential growth" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/300px-Population_curve.svg.png" width="300" height="177" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/450px-Population_curve.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Population_curve.svg/600px-Population_curve.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="325" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Population_curve.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Graph showing human population growth from 10,000 BC&#160;– 2000 AD, illustrating current exponential growth</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WorldPopGrowth.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/WorldPopGrowth.png/300px-WorldPopGrowth.png" width="300" height="231" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/WorldPopGrowth.png/450px-WorldPopGrowth.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/WorldPopGrowth.png/600px-WorldPopGrowth.png 2x" data-file-width="979" data-file-height="754" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WorldPopGrowth.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
World population growth rate, 1950–2050, as estimated in 2011 by the U.S. Census Bureau, International Data Base</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>According to the 2008 Revision of the official United Nations population estimates and projections, the <a href="/wiki/World_population" title="World population">world population</a> is projected to reach 7 billion early in 2012, up from the current 6.9 billion (May 2009), to exceed 9 billion people by 2050. Most of the increase will be in <a href="/wiki/Developing_country" title="Developing country">developing countries</a> whose population is projected to rise from 5.6 billion in 2009 to 7.9 billion in 2050. This increase will be distributed among the population aged 15–59 (1.2 billion) and 60 or over (1.1 billion) because the number of children under age 15 in developing countries is predicted to decrease. In contrast, the population of the more <a href="/wiki/Developed_country" title="Developed country">developed regions</a> is expected to undergo only slight increase from 1.23 billion to 1.28 billion, and this would have declined to 1.15 billion but for a projected net migration from developing to developed countries, which is expected to average 2.4 million persons annually from 2009 to 2050.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Long-term estimates in 2004 of global population suggest a peak at around 2070 of nine to ten billion people, and then a slow decrease to 8.4 billion by 2100.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<p>Emerging economies like those of China and India aspire to the living standards of the Western world as does the non-industrialized world in general.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> It is the combination of population increase in the developing world and unsustainable consumption levels in the developed world that poses a stark challenge to sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2006_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2006-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Carrying_capacity">Carrying capacity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Carrying capacity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">Carrying capacity</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Graph comparing the Ecological Footprint of different nations with their Human Development Index" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg/300px-Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg" width="300" height="184" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg/450px-Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f1/Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg/600px-Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg 2x" data-file-width="850" data-file-height="522" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Human_welfare_and_ecological_footprint_sustainability.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>Ecological footprint for different nations compared to their Human Development Index (HDI)</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>At the global scale, scientific data now indicates that humans are living beyond the <a href="/wiki/Carrying_capacity" title="Carrying capacity">carrying capacity</a> of planet Earth and that this cannot continue indefinitely. This scientific evidence comes from many sources but is presented in detail in the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Ecosystem_Assessment" title="Millennium Ecosystem Assessment">Millennium Ecosystem Assessment</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Planetary_boundaries" title="Planetary boundaries">planetary boundaries</a> framework.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> An early detailed examination of global limits was published in the 1972 book <i><a href="/wiki/Limits_to_Growth" class="mw-redirect" title="Limits to Growth">Limits to Growth</a></i>, which has prompted follow-up commentary and analysis.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> A 2012 review in <i>Nature</i> by 22 international researchers expressed concerns that the Earth may be "approaching a state shift" in its biosphere.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">Ecological footprint</a> measures human consumption in terms of the biologically productive land needed to provide the resources, and absorb the wastes of the average global citizen. In 2008 it required 2.7 <a href="/wiki/Global_hectare" title="Global hectare">global hectares</a> per person, 30% more than the natural biological capacity of 2.1 global hectares (assuming no provision for other organisms).<sup id="cite_ref-LPR_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LPR-56">[56]</a></sup> The resulting <a href="/wiki/Ecological_deficit" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecological deficit">ecological deficit</a> must be met from unsustainable <i>extra</i> sources and these are obtained in three ways: embedded in the goods and services of world trade; taken from the past (e.g. <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>); or borrowed from the future as unsustainable resource usage (e.g. by <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">over exploiting</a> <a href="/wiki/Forests#Forest_management_and_forest_loss" class="mw-redirect" title="Forests">forests</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fisheries_management" title="Fisheries management">fisheries</a>).</p>
<p>The figure (right) examines sustainability at the scale of individual countries by contrasting their Ecological Footprint with their UN <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> (a measure of standard of living). The graph shows what is necessary for countries to maintain an acceptable standard of living for their citizens while, at the same time, maintaining sustainable resource use. The general trend is for higher standards of living to become less sustainable. As always, <a href="/wiki/Population_growth" title="Population growth">population growth</a> has a marked influence on levels of consumption and the efficiency of resource use.<sup id="cite_ref-Ehrlich.26Holden_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ehrlich.26Holden-68">[68]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> The sustainability goal is to raise the global standard of living without increasing the use of resources beyond globally sustainable levels; that is, to not exceed "one planet" consumption. Information generated by reports at the national, regional and city scales confirm the global trend towards societies that are becoming less sustainable over time.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Romanian_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Romanian American">Romanian American</a> economist <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Georgescu-Roegen" title="Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen">Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen</a>, a <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_considered_father_or_mother_of_a_scientific_field" title="List of people considered father or mother of a scientific field">progenitor</a> in <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Paradigm_shift#Kuhnian_paradigm_shifts" title="Paradigm shift">paradigm founder</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a>, has argued that the carrying capacity of Earth — that is, Earth's capacity to sustain human populations and consumption levels — is bound to decrease sometime in the future as Earth's finite stock of mineral resources is presently being extracted and put to use.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:303</sup> Leading ecological economist and <a href="/wiki/Steady-state_economy#Herman_Daly.27s_concept_of_a_steady-state_economy" title="Steady-state economy">steady-state theorist</a> <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">Herman Daly</a>, a student of Georgescu-Roegen, has <a href="/wiki/Nicholas_Georgescu-Roegen#Daly.27s_concession" title="Nicholas Georgescu-Roegen">propounded the same argument</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">:369–371</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_human_impact_on_biodiversity">Global human impact on biodiversity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Global human impact on biodiversity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Ecosystem_Assessment" title="Millennium Ecosystem Assessment">Millennium Ecosystem Assessment</a></div>
<p>At a fundamental level <a href="/wiki/Energy_flow_(ecology)" title="Energy flow (ecology)">energy flow</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biogeochemical_cycle" title="Biogeochemical cycle">biogeochemical cycling</a> set an upper limit on the number and mass of organisms in any ecosystem.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Human impacts on the Earth are demonstrated in a general way through detrimental changes in the global biogeochemical cycles of chemicals that are critical to life, most notably those of <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oxygen" title="Oxygen">oxygen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon" title="Carbon">carbon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen" title="Nitrogen">nitrogen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phosphorus" title="Phosphorus">phosphorus</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <i>Millennium Ecosystem Assessment</i> is an international synthesis by over 1000 of the world's leading biological scientists that analyzes the state of the Earth’s <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a> and provides summaries and guidelines for decision-makers. It concludes that human activity is having a significant and escalating impact on the biodiversity of world <a href="/wiki/Ecosystems" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecosystems">ecosystems</a>, reducing both their <a href="/wiki/Resilience_(ecology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Resilience (ecology)">resilience</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biocapacity" title="Biocapacity">biocapacity</a>. The report refers to natural systems as humanity's "life-support system", providing essential "<a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a>". The assessment measures 24 ecosystem services concluding that only four have shown improvement over the last 50 years, 15 are in serious decline, and five are in a precarious condition.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_development_goals">Sustainable development goals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Sustainable development goals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development_goals" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable development goals">Sustainable development goals</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> (SDGs) are the current harmonized set of seventeen future international development targets.</p>
<p>The Official Agenda for Sustainable Development adopted on 25 September 2015 has 92 paragraphs, with the main paragraph (51) outlining the 17 Sustainable Development Goals and its associated 169 targets. This included the following seventeen goals:<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li><b>Poverty</b> – <a href="/wiki/End_poverty_in_all_its_forms_everywhere" class="mw-redirect" title="End poverty in all its forms everywhere">End poverty in all its forms everywhere</a><sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Food</b> – <a href="/wiki/Hunger#Global_initiatives_to_end_hunger" title="Hunger">End hunger</a>, achieve <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> and improved nutrition and promote <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a><sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Health</b> – Ensure healthy lives and promote <a href="/wiki/Well-being" title="Well-being">well-being</a> for all at all ages<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Education</b> – Ensure <a href="/wiki/Inclusion_(education)" title="Inclusion (education)">inclusive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Educational_equity" title="Educational equity">equitable</a> quality education and promote <a href="/wiki/Lifelong_learning" title="Lifelong learning">lifelong learning</a> opportunities for all<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Women</b> – Achieve <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> and <a href="/wiki/Empowerment" title="Empowerment">empower</a> all women and girls<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Water</b> – Ensure availability and sustainable management of water and sanitation for all<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Energy</b> – Ensure access to affordable, reliable, sustainable and modern energy for all<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Economy</b> – Promote sustained, inclusive and sustainable economic growth, full and productive employment and <a href="/wiki/Decent_work" title="Decent work">decent work</a> for all<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Infrastructure</b> – Build resilient <a href="/wiki/Infrastructure" title="Infrastructure">infrastructure</a>, promote inclusive and <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_industries" title="Sustainable industries">sustainable industrialization</a> and foster <a href="/wiki/Innovation" title="Innovation">innovation</a><sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Inequality</b> – Reduce <a href="/wiki/Social_inequality" title="Social inequality">inequality</a> within and among countries<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Habitation</b> – Make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient and sustainable<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Consumption</b> – Ensure sustainable consumption and production patterns<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Climate</b> – Take urgent action to combat <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and its impacts<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Marine-ecosystems</b> – Conserve and sustainably use the oceans, seas and marine <a href="/wiki/Resource" title="Resource">resources</a> for sustainable development<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Ecosystems</b> – Protect, restore and promote sustainable use of terrestrial <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, sustainably manage forests, combat <a href="/wiki/Desertification" title="Desertification">desertification</a>, and halt and reverse land degradation and halt <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity_loss" title="Biodiversity loss">biodiversity loss</a><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Institutions</b> – Promote peaceful and inclusive societies for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>, provide <a href="/wiki/Right_to_fair_trial" class="mw-redirect" title="Right to fair trial">access to justice</a> for all and build effective, accountable and inclusive institutions at all levels<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Sustainability</b> – Strengthen the means of implementation and revitalize the global partnership for <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>As of August 2015, there were 169 proposed targets for these goals and 304 proposed indicators to show compliance.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> (SDGs) replace the eight <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a> (MDGs), which expired at the end of 2015. The MDGs were established in 2000 following the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Summit" title="Millennium Summit">Millennium Summit</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>. Adopted by the 189 <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_member_states" class="mw-redirect" title="United Nations member states">United Nations member states</a> at the time and more than twenty <a href="/wiki/International_organizations" class="mw-redirect" title="International organizations">international organizations</a>, these goals were advanced to help achieve the following <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> standards by 2015.</p>
<ol>
<li>To eradicate <a href="/wiki/Extreme_poverty" title="Extreme poverty">extreme poverty and hunger</a></li>
<li>To achieve <a href="/wiki/Universal_primary_education" class="mw-redirect" title="Universal primary education">universal primary education</a></li>
<li>To promote <a href="/wiki/Gender_equality" title="Gender equality">gender equality</a> and empower women</li>
<li>To reduce <a href="/wiki/Child_mortality" title="Child mortality">child mortality</a></li>
<li>To improve <a href="/wiki/Maternal_health" title="Maternal health">maternal health</a></li>
<li>To combat <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a>, and other diseases</li>
<li>To ensure environmental sustainability (one of the targets in this goal focuses on increasing sustainable access to safe <a href="/wiki/Water_supply" title="Water supply">drinking water</a> and basic <a href="/wiki/Sanitation" title="Sanitation">sanitation</a>)</li>
<li>To develop a global partnership for development</li>
</ol>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sustainable_development">Sustainable development</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Sustainable development">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to the data that member countries represented to the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cuba" title="Cuba">Cuba</a> was the only nation in the world in 2006 that met the <a href="/wiki/World_Wide_Fund_for_Nature" title="World Wide Fund for Nature">World Wide Fund for Nature</a>'s definition of <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a>, with an <a href="/wiki/Ecological_footprint" title="Ecological footprint">ecological footprint</a> of less than 1.8 hectares per capita, 1.5, and a <a href="/wiki/Human_Development_Index" title="Human Development Index">Human Development Index</a> of over 0.8, 0.855.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_dimension">Environmental dimension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Environmental dimension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Healthy ecosystems provide vital goods and services to humans and other organisms. There are two major ways of reducing negative human impact and enhancing <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> and the first of these is <a href="/wiki/Environmental_management" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental management">environmental management</a>. This direct approach is based largely on information gained from <a href="/wiki/Earth_science" title="Earth science">earth science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_science" title="Environmental science">environmental science</a> and <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation biology</a>. However, this is management at the end of a long series of indirect causal factors that are initiated by human <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a>, so a second approach is through demand management of human resource use.</p>
<p>Management of human consumption of resources is an indirect approach based largely on information gained from <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>. Herman Daly has suggested three broad criteria for ecological sustainability: renewable resources should provide a <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_yield" title="Sustainable yield">sustainable yield</a> (the rate of harvest should not exceed the rate of regeneration); for non-renewable resources there should be equivalent development of renewable substitutes; waste generation should not exceed the assimilative capacity of the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Environmental_management">Environmental management</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Environmental management">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sustainability_and_environmental_management" title="Sustainability and environmental management">Sustainability and environmental management</a></div>
<p>At the global scale and in the broadest sense environmental management involves the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a>, <a href="/wiki/Freshwater" class="mw-redirect" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> systems, land and <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmosphere</a>, but following the sustainability principle of scale it can be equally applied to any ecosystem from a tropical rainforest to a home garden.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Atmosphere">Atmosphere</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Atmosphere">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>At a March 2009 meeting of the <a href="/wiki/Copenhagen_Climate_Council" title="Copenhagen Climate Council">Copenhagen Climate Council</a>, 2,500 climate experts from 80 countries issued a keynote statement that there is now "no excuse" for failing to act on global warming and that without strong carbon reduction "abrupt or irreversible" shifts in climate may occur that "will be very difficult for contemporary societies to cope with".<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup> Management of the global atmosphere now involves assessment of all aspects of the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> to identify opportunities to address human-induced <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> and this has become a major focus of scientific research because of the potential catastrophic effects on biodiversity and human communities (see <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">Energy</a> below).</p>
<p>Other human impacts on the atmosphere include the <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution" title="Air pollution">air pollution</a> in cities, the <a href="/wiki/Pollutants" class="mw-redirect" title="Pollutants">pollutants</a> including toxic chemicals like <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxides" class="mw-redirect" title="Nitrogen oxides">nitrogen oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur oxides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a> and <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Atmospheric particulate matter">airborne particulate matter</a> that produce <a href="/wiki/Photochemical_smog" class="mw-redirect" title="Photochemical smog">photochemical smog</a> and <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbon" title="Chlorofluorocarbon">chlorofluorocarbons</a> that degrade the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>. <a href="/wiki/Human_impact_on_the_environment" title="Human impact on the environment">Anthropogenic</a> particulates such as sulfate <a href="/wiki/Aerosol" title="Aerosol">aerosols</a> in the atmosphere reduce the direct <a href="/wiki/Irradiance" title="Irradiance">irradiance</a> and reflectance (<a href="/wiki/Albedo" title="Albedo">albedo</a>) of the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>'s surface. Known as <a href="/wiki/Global_dimming" title="Global dimming">global dimming</a>, the decrease is estimated to have been about 4% between 1960 and 1990 although the trend has subsequently reversed. Global dimming may have disturbed the global <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> by reducing evaporation and rainfall in some areas. It also creates a cooling effect and this may have partially masked the effect of <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gases" class="mw-redirect" title="Greenhouse gases">greenhouse gases</a> on <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Freshwater_and_oceans">Freshwater and oceans</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Freshwater and oceans">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bleached_coral.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bleached_coral.jpg/220px-Bleached_coral.jpg" width="220" height="171" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bleached_coral.jpg/330px-Bleached_coral.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Bleached_coral.jpg/440px-Bleached_coral.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="467" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bleached_coral.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Changes in environmental conditions lead to <a href="/wiki/Coral_bleaching" title="Coral bleaching">coral bleaching</a> and harm to biodiversity of fragile marine ecosystems.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Water covers 71% of the Earth's surface. Of this, 97.5% is the salty water of the <a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">oceans</a> and only 2.5% freshwater, most of which is locked up in the <a href="/wiki/Antarctic_ice_sheet" title="Antarctic ice sheet">Antarctic ice sheet</a>. The remaining freshwater is found in glaciers, lakes, rivers, wetlands, the soil, aquifers and atmosphere. Due to the water cycle, fresh water supply is continually replenished by precipitation, however there is still a limited amount necessitating management of this resource. Awareness of the global importance of preserving water for <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> has only recently emerged as, during the 20th century, more than half the world’s <a href="/wiki/Wetlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Wetlands">wetlands</a> have been lost along with their valuable environmental services. Increasing <a href="/wiki/Urbanization" title="Urbanization">urbanization</a> pollutes clean water supplies and much of the world still does not have access to clean, safe water.<sup id="cite_ref-Atlas_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atlas-115">[115]</a></sup> Greater emphasis is now being placed on the improved management of blue (harvestable) and green (soil water available for plant use) water, and this applies at all scales of water management.<sup id="cite_ref-water_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-water-116">[116]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ocean" title="Ocean">Ocean</a> circulation patterns have a strong influence on <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> and, in turn, the food supply of both humans and other organisms. Scientists have warned of the possibility, under the influence of climate change, of a sudden alteration in circulation patterns of <a href="/wiki/Ocean_current" title="Ocean current">ocean currents</a> that could drastically alter the climate in some regions of the globe.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup> Ten per cent of the world's population&#160;– about 600 million people&#160;– live in low-lying areas vulnerable to sea level rise.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Land_use">Land use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Land use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rice_Field.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A farmer working in a rice paddy." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Rice_Field.jpg/220px-Rice_Field.jpg" width="220" height="195" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bd/Rice_Field.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="315" data-file-height="279" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rice_Field.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A rice paddy in Bangladesh. Rice, wheat, corn and potatoes make up more than half the world's food supply.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Loss of biodiversity stems largely from the habitat loss and fragmentation produced by the human appropriation of land for development, forestry and agriculture as <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">natural capital</a> is progressively converted to man-made capital. Land use change is fundamental to the operations of the <a href="/wiki/Biosphere" title="Biosphere">biosphere</a> because alterations in the relative proportions of land dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Urbanisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Urbanisation">urbanisation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Woodland" title="Woodland">woodland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pasture" title="Pasture">pasture</a> have a marked effect on the global water, carbon and nitrogen biogeochemical cycles and this can impact negatively on both natural and human systems.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> At the local human scale, major sustainability benefits accrue from <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_gardening" title="Sustainable gardening">sustainable parks and gardens</a> and <a href="/wiki/Green_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Green cities">green cities</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">[120]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic_Revolution" title="Neolithic Revolution">Neolithic Revolution</a> about 47% of the world’s forests have been lost to human use. Present-day forests occupy about a quarter of the world’s ice-free land with about half of these occurring in the tropics.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> In temperate and boreal regions forest area is gradually increasing (with the exception of Siberia), but <a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">deforestation</a> in the tropics is of major concern.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">Food</a> is essential to life. Feeding more than seven billion human bodies takes a heavy toll on the Earth’s resources. This begins with the appropriation of about 38% of the Earth’s land surface<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> and about 20% of its net primary productivity.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Added to this are the resource-hungry activities of industrial agribusiness&#160;– everything from the crop need for irrigation water, synthetic <a href="/wiki/Fertilizer" title="Fertilizer">fertilizers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> to the resource costs of food packaging, transport (now a major part of global trade) and retail. Environmental problems associated with <a href="/wiki/Industrial_agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial agriculture">industrial agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Agribusiness" title="Agribusiness">agribusiness</a> are now being addressed through such movements as sustainable agriculture, <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a> and more sustainable business practices.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Management_of_human_consumption">Management of human consumption</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Management of human consumption">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">Consumption (economics)</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Helix_of_sustainability.png" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing ways that the manufacturing process can reduce the use of energy" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/Helix_of_sustainability.png/220px-Helix_of_sustainability.png" width="220" height="176" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/Helix_of_sustainability.png/330px-Helix_of_sustainability.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/13/Helix_of_sustainability.png/440px-Helix_of_sustainability.png 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="1024" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Helix_of_sustainability.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Helix of sustainability&#160;– the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a> of manufacturing</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The underlying driver of direct human impacts on the environment is human consumption.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> This impact is reduced by not only consuming less but by also making the full cycle of production, use and disposal more sustainable.
