Consumption of goods and services can be analysed and managed at all scales through the chain of consumption, starting with the effects of individual lifestyle choices and spending patterns, through to the resource demands of specific goods and services, the impacts of economic sectors, through national economies to the global economy.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> Analysis of consumption patterns relates resource use to the environmental, social and economic impacts at the scale or context under investigation. The ideas of embodied resource use (the total resources needed to produce a product or service), <a href="/wiki/Resource_intensity" title="Resource intensity">resource intensity</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Resource_productivity" title="Resource productivity">resource productivity</a> are important tools for understanding the impacts of consumption. Key resource categories relating to human needs are <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">energy</a>, materials and water.</p>
<p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>, hosted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP), published the first global scientific assessment on the impacts of consumption and production<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup> and identified priority actions for developed and developing countries. The study found that the most critical impacts are related to <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a> health, human health and <a href="/wiki/Resource_depletion" title="Resource depletion">resource depletion</a>. From a production perspective, it found that fossil-fuel combustion processes, agriculture and <a href="/wiki/Fisheries" class="mw-redirect" title="Fisheries">fisheries</a> have the most important impacts. Meanwhile, from a final <a href="/wiki/Consumption_(economics)" title="Consumption (economics)">consumption</a> perspective, it found that household consumption related to mobility, shelter, <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">food</a> and energy-using products cause the majority of <a href="/wiki/Life_cycle_assessment" class="mw-redirect" title="Life cycle assessment">life-cycle</a> impacts of consumption.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Energy">Energy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Energy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_energy" title="Sustainable energy">Sustainable energy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">Renewable energy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Efficient_energy_use" title="Efficient energy use">Efficient energy use</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Diagram showing the flow of CO2 in an ecosystem" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg/220px-Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg/330px-Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg/440px-Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="563" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Genomics_GTL_Program_Payoffs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Flow of CO<sub>2</sub> in an <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystem</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The Sun's energy, stored by plants (<a href="/wiki/Primary_producer" class="mw-redirect" title="Primary producer">primary producers</a>) during <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a>, passes through the <a href="/wiki/Food_chain" title="Food chain">food chain</a> to other organisms to ultimately power all living processes. Since the <a href="/wiki/Industrial_revolution" class="mw-redirect" title="Industrial revolution">industrial revolution</a> the concentrated energy of the <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">Sun</a> stored in fossilized plants as <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuel" title="Fossil fuel">fossil fuels</a> has been a major driver of <a href="/wiki/Technology" title="Technology">technology</a> which, in turn, has been the source of both economic and political power. In 2007 climate scientists of the <a href="/wiki/Intergovernmental_Panel_on_Climate_Change" title="Intergovernmental Panel on Climate Change">IPCC</a> concluded that there was at least a 90% probability that atmospheric increase in CO<sub>2</sub> was human-induced, mostly as a result of fossil fuel emissions but, to a lesser extent from changes in land use. Stabilizing the world’s climate will require high-income countries to reduce their emissions by 60–90% over 2006 levels by 2050 which should hold CO<sub>2</sub> levels at 450–650&#160;ppm from current levels of about 380&#160;ppm. Above this level, temperatures could rise by more than 2&#160;°C to produce “catastrophic” <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Reduction of current CO<sub>2</sub> levels must be achieved against a background of global population increase and developing countries aspiring to energy-intensive high consumption Western lifestyles.<sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reducing greenhouse emissions, is being tackled at all scales, ranging from tracking the passage of carbon through the <a href="/wiki/Carbon_cycle" title="Carbon cycle">carbon cycle</a><sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[132]</a></sup> to the <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy_commercialization" title="Renewable energy commercialization">commercialization of renewable energy</a>, developing less carbon-hungry technology and transport systems and attempts by individuals to lead <a href="/wiki/Carbon_neutral" class="mw-redirect" title="Carbon neutral">carbon neutral</a> lifestyles by monitoring the fossil fuel use embodied in all the goods and services they use.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Engineering" title="Engineering">Engineering</a> of <a href="/wiki/Emerging_technologies" title="Emerging technologies">emerging technologies</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Carbon-neutral_fuel" title="Carbon-neutral fuel">carbon-neutral fuel</a><sup id="cite_ref-Graves2011rev_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Graves2011rev-134">[134]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pearson2012_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2012-135">[135]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup> and energy storage systems such as <a href="/wiki/Power_to_gas" title="Power to gas">power to gas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Compressed_air_energy_storage" title="Compressed air energy storage">compressed air energy storage</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Pumped-storage_hydroelectricity" title="Pumped-storage hydroelectricity">pumped-storage hydroelectricity</a><sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[140]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> are necessary to store power from transient <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a> sources including emerging renewables such as <a href="/wiki/Airborne_wind_turbine" title="Airborne wind turbine">airborne wind turbines</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Water">Water</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Water">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Water_resources" title="Water resources">Water resources</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Water_security" title="Water security">Water security</a> and <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a> are inextricably linked. In the decade 1951–60 human water withdrawals were four times greater than the previous decade. This rapid increase resulted from scientific and technological developments impacting through the <a href="/wiki/Economy" title="Economy">economy</a>&#160;– especially the increase in irrigated land, growth in industrial and power sectors, and intensive <a href="/wiki/Dam" title="Dam">dam</a> construction on all continents. This altered the water cycle of <a href="/wiki/River" title="River">rivers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lake" title="Lake">lakes</a>, affected their <a href="/wiki/Water_quality" title="Water quality">water quality</a> and had a significant impact on the global water cycle.<sup id="cite_ref-Shik_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shik-143">[143]</a></sup> Currently towards 35% of human water use is unsustainable, drawing on diminishing aquifers and reducing the flows of major rivers: this percentage is likely to increase if <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> impacts become more severe, <a href="/wiki/Population" title="Population">populations</a> increase, aquifers become progressively depleted and supplies become polluted and unsanitary.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> From 1961 to 2001 water demand doubled&#160;— agricultural use increased by 75%, industrial use by more than 200%, and domestic use more than 400%.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> In the 1990s it was estimated that humans were using 40–50% of the globally available freshwater in the approximate proportion of 70% for agriculture, 22% for <a href="/wiki/Industry" title="Industry">industry</a>, and 8% for domestic purposes with total use progressively increasing.<sup id="cite_ref-Shik_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shik-143">[143]</a></sup></p>
<p>Water efficiency is being improved on a global scale by increased <a href="/wiki/Demand_management" title="Demand management">demand management</a>, improved infrastructure, improved water <a href="/wiki/Resource_productivity" title="Resource productivity">productivity</a> of agriculture, minimising the water intensity (embodied water) of goods and services, addressing shortages in the non-industrialized world, concentrating food production in areas of high productivity, and planning for <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a>, such as through flexible system design. A promising direction towards sustainable development is to design systems that are flexible and reversible.<sup id="cite_ref-tandfonline.com_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tandfonline.com-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Zhang_2012_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zhang_2012-5">[5]</a></sup> At the local level, people are becoming more self-sufficient by harvesting rainwater and reducing use of mains water.<sup id="cite_ref-water_116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-water-116">[116]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Food">Food</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Food">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Feijoada_01.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Feijoada_01.jpg/220px-Feijoada_01.jpg" width="220" height="172" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/84/Feijoada_01.jpg/330px-Feijoada_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/84/Feijoada_01.jpg 2x" data-file-width="342" data-file-height="268" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Feijoada_01.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Feijoada&#160;— A typical black bean food dish from <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Food" title="Food">Food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">Food security</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Category:Sustainable_food_system" title="Category:Sustainable food system">Category:Sustainable food system</a></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/American_Public_Health_Association" title="American Public Health Association">American Public Health Association</a> (APHA) defines a "sustainable food system"<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> as "one that provides healthy food to meet current food needs while maintaining healthy ecosystems that can also provide food for generations to come with minimal negative impact to the environment. A sustainable food system also encourages local production and distribution infrastructures and makes nutritious food available, accessible, and affordable to all. Further, it is humane and just, protecting farmers and other workers, consumers, and communities."<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> Concerns about the environmental impacts of agribusiness and the stark contrast between the <a href="/wiki/Obesity" title="Obesity">obesity</a> problems of the Western world and the poverty and food insecurity of the developing world have generated a strong movement towards healthy, sustainable eating as a major component of overall <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumerism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup> The environmental effects of different dietary patterns depend on many factors, including the proportion of animal and plant foods consumed and the method of food production.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">[154]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> has published a <i>Global Strategy on Diet, Physical Activity and Health</i> report which was endorsed by the May 2004 <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">World Health Assembly</a>. It recommends the Mediterranean diet which is associated with health and <a href="/wiki/Longevity" title="Longevity">longevity</a> and is low in <a href="/wiki/Meat" title="Meat">meat</a>, rich in <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruits</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetables</a>, low in added sugar and limited salt, and low in <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fatty</a> acids; the traditional source of <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a> in the Mediterranean is <a href="/wiki/Olive_oil" title="Olive oil">olive oil</a>, rich in <a href="/wiki/Monounsaturated_fat" title="Monounsaturated fat">monounsaturated fat</a>. The healthy rice-based Japanese diet is also high in <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a> and low in fat. Both diets are low in meat and <a href="/wiki/Saturated_fat" title="Saturated fat">saturated fats</a> and high in <a href="/wiki/Legume" title="Legume">legumes</a> and other vegetables; they are associated with a low incidence of ailments and low environmental impact.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">[155]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the global level the environmental impact of agribusiness is being addressed through <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organic_farming" title="Organic farming">organic farming</a>. At the local level there are various movements working towards local food production, more productive use of urban wastelands and domestic gardens including <a href="/wiki/Permaculture" title="Permaculture">permaculture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urban_horticulture" title="Urban horticulture">urban horticulture</a>, <a href="/wiki/Local_food" title="Local food">local food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slow_food" class="mw-redirect" title="Slow food">slow food</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_gardening" title="Sustainable gardening">sustainable gardening</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Organic_gardening" class="mw-redirect" title="Organic gardening">organic gardening</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[156]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[157]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sustainable_seafood" title="Sustainable seafood">Sustainable seafood</a> is seafood from either fished or farmed sources that can maintain or increase production in the future without jeopardizing the ecosystems from which it was acquired. The sustainable seafood movement has gained momentum as more people become aware about both <a href="/wiki/Overfishing" title="Overfishing">overfishing</a> and environmentally destructive fishing methods.</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Materials.2C_toxic_substances.2C_waste">Materials, toxic substances, waste</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Materials, toxic substances, waste">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg/280px-Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg" width="280" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg/420px-Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg/560px-Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2352" data-file-height="1568" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Electric_wire_reel_reused_in_a_furniture_ecodesign.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
An electric wire reel reused as a center table in a <a href="/wiki/Rio_de_Janeiro" title="Rio de Janeiro">Rio de Janeiro</a> <a href="/wiki/Interior_design" title="Interior design">decoration</a> <a href="/wiki/Fair" title="Fair">fair</a>. The reuse of materials is a sustainable practice that is rapidly growing among <a href="/wiki/Designers" class="mw-redirect" title="Designers">designers</a> in <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As global population and affluence has increased, so has the use of various materials increased in volume, diversity and distance transported. Included here are raw materials, minerals, synthetic chemicals (including <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_substances" class="mw-redirect" title="Hazardous substances">hazardous substances</a>), manufactured products, food, living organisms and waste.<sup id="cite_ref-VITAL_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VITAL-158">[158]</a></sup> By 2050, humanity could consume an estimated 140 billion tons of minerals, ores, fossil fuels and biomass per year (three times its current amount) unless the economic growth rate is decoupled from the rate of natural resource consumption. Developed countries' citizens consume an average of 16 tons of those four key resources per capita, ranging up to 40 or more tons per person in some developed countries with resource consumption levels far beyond what is likely sustainable.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">[159]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sustainable use of materials has targeted the idea of <a href="/wiki/Dematerialization_(economics)" title="Dematerialization (economics)">dematerialization</a>, converting the linear path of materials (extraction, use, disposal in landfill) to a <a href="/wiki/Material_flow_accounting" title="Material flow accounting">circular material flow</a> that reuses materials as much as possible, much like the cycling and reuse of waste in nature.<sup id="cite_ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> This approach is supported by <a href="/wiki/Product_stewardship" title="Product stewardship">product stewardship</a> and the increasing use of <a href="/wiki/Material_flow_analysis" title="Material flow analysis">material flow analysis</a> at all levels, especially individual countries and the global economy.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[161]</a></sup> The use of sustainable biomaterials that come from renewable sources and that can be recycled is preferred to the use on non-renewables from a life cycle standpoint.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Waste_hierarchy.svg" class="image"><img alt="Pyramid diagram showing ways of dealing with waste with the most important ones towards the top" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Waste_hierarchy.svg/220px-Waste_hierarchy.svg.png" width="220" height="120" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Waste_hierarchy.svg/330px-Waste_hierarchy.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Waste_hierarchy.svg/440px-Waste_hierarchy.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="550" data-file-height="300" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Waste_hierarchy.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<center>The <a href="/wiki/Waste_hierarchy" title="Waste hierarchy">waste hierarchy</a></center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Synthetic_chemical" class="mw-redirect" title="Synthetic chemical">Synthetic chemical</a> production has escalated following the stimulus it received during the second World War. Chemical production includes everything from herbicides, pesticides and fertilizers to domestic chemicals and hazardous substances.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">[162]</a></sup> Apart from the build-up of greenhouse gas emissions in the atmosphere, chemicals of particular concern include: <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metals" title="Heavy metals">heavy metals</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_waste" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear waste">nuclear waste</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">chlorofluorocarbons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutants" class="mw-redirect" title="Persistent organic pollutants">persistent organic pollutants</a> and all harmful chemicals capable of <a href="/wiki/Bioaccumulation" title="Bioaccumulation">bioaccumulation</a>. Although most synthetic chemicals are harmless there needs to be rigorous testing of new chemicals, in all countries, for adverse environmental and health effects. International legislation has been established to deal with the global distribution and management of <a href="/wiki/Dangerous_goods" title="Dangerous goods">dangerous goods</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup> The effects of some chemical agents needed long-term measurements and a lot of legal battles to realize their danger to human health. The classification of the toxic carcinogenic agents is handle by the <a href="/wiki/IARC_Group_1_carcinogen" class="mw-redirect" title="IARC Group 1 carcinogen">International Agency for Research on Cancer</a>.</p>
<p>Every economic activity produces material that can be classified as waste. To reduce waste, industry, business and government are now mimicking nature by turning the waste produced by <a href="/wiki/Industrial_metabolism" title="Industrial metabolism">industrial metabolism</a> into resource. Dematerialization is being encouraged through the ideas of <a href="/wiki/Industrial_ecology" title="Industrial ecology">industrial ecology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ecodesign" title="Ecodesign">ecodesign</a><sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[165]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Ecolabelling" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecolabelling">ecolabelling</a>. In addition to the well-established “reduce, reuse and recycle,” shoppers are using their purchasing power for <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumerism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brower_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brower-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The European Union is expected to table by the end of 2015 an ambitious Circular Economy package which is expected to include concrete legislative proposals on waste management, <a href="/wiki/Ecodesign" title="Ecodesign">ecodesign</a> and limits on land fills.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_dimension">Economic dimension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economic dimension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">Ecological economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">Environmental economics</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Green_economy" title="Green economy">Green economy</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Reproduction of painting The Great Fish Market, painted by Jan Brueghel the Elder" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg/220px-Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg" width="220" height="142" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg/330px-Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bf/Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg/440px-Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2859" data-file-height="1851" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder-Great_Fish_market.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <i>Great Fish Market</i>, painted by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Brueghel_the_Elder" title="Jan Brueghel the Elder">Jan Brueghel the Elder</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On one account, sustainability "concerns the specification of a set of actions to be taken by present persons that will not diminish the prospects of future persons to enjoy levels of consumption, wealth, utility, or welfare comparable to those enjoyed by present persons."<sup id="cite_ref-Bromley_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bromley-166">[166]</a></sup> Sustainability interfaces with economics through the social and ecological consequences of economic activity.<sup id="cite_ref-Daly_.26_Cobb_1989_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daly_.26_Cobb_1989-29">[29]</a></sup> Sustainability economics represents: "...&#160;a broad interpretation of ecological economics where environmental and ecological variables and issues are basic but part of a multidimensional perspective. Social, cultural, health-related and monetary/financial aspects have to be integrated into the analysis."<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[167]</a></sup> However, the concept of sustainability is much broader than the concepts of sustained yield of welfare, resources, or profit margins.<sup id="cite_ref-168" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-168">[168]</a></sup> At present, the average per capita consumption of people in the developing world is sustainable but population numbers are increasing and individuals are aspiring to high-consumption Western lifestyles. The developed world population is only increasing slightly but consumption levels are unsustainable. The challenge for sustainability is to curb and manage Western consumption while raising the standard of living of the developing world without increasing its resource use and environmental impact. This must be done by using strategies and technology that break the link between, on the one hand, economic growth and on the other, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_damage" class="mw-redirect" title="Environmental damage">environmental damage</a> and resource depletion.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[169]</a></sup></p>
<p>A recent UNEP report proposes a <a href="/wiki/Green_economy" title="Green economy">green economy</a> defined as one that “improves human well-being and social equity, while significantly reducing environmental risks and ecological scarcities”: it "does not favor one political perspective over another but works to minimize excessive depletion of <a href="/wiki/Natural_capital" title="Natural capital">natural capital</a>". The report makes three key findings: “that greening not only generates increases in wealth, in particular a gain in ecological commons or natural capital, but also (over a period of six years) produces a higher rate of GDP growth”; that there is “an inextricable link between poverty eradication and better maintenance and conservation of the ecological commons, arising from the benefit flows from natural capital that are received directly by the poor”; "in the transition to a green economy, new jobs are created, which in time exceed the losses in “brown economy” jobs. However, there is a period of job losses in transition, which requires investment in re-skilling and re-educating the workforce”.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several key areas have been targeted for economic analysis and reform: the environmental effects of unconstrained economic growth; the consequences of nature being treated as an economic <a href="/wiki/Externality" title="Externality">externality</a>; and the possibility of an economics that takes greater account of the social and environmental consequences of market behavior.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decoupling_environmental_degradation_and_economic_growth">Decoupling environmental degradation and economic growth</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Decoupling environmental degradation and economic growth">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">Ecological economics</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Ephemeralization" title="Ephemeralization">Ephemeralization</a></div>

</tr>
<tr>
<td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">
<ul>

<li><a href="/wiki/Green_politics" title="Green politics">Green politics</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pollution_haven_hypothesis" title="Pollution haven hypothesis">Pollution haven hypothesis</a></li>
</ul>
</td>
</tr>
