<tr>
<td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%">
<div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
<ul>
<li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Green_economics_sidebar" title="Template talk:Green economics sidebar"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li>
<li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Green_economics_sidebar&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li>
</ul>
</div>
</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>Historically there has been a close correlation between <a href="/wiki/Economic_growth" title="Economic growth">economic growth</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_degradation" title="Environmental degradation">environmental degradation</a>: as communities grow, so the environment declines. This trend is clearly demonstrated on graphs of human population numbers, economic growth, and environmental indicators.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup> Unsustainable economic growth has been starkly compared to the malignant growth of a cancer<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">[173]</a></sup> because it eats away at the Earth's <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> which are its life-support system. There is concern that, unless resource use is checked, modern global civilization will follow the path of ancient civilizations that collapsed through <a href="/wiki/Overexploitation" title="Overexploitation">overexploitation</a> of their resource base.<sup id="cite_ref-collapse_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collapse-174">[174]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">[175]</a></sup> While conventional economics is concerned largely with economic growth and the efficient allocation of resources, ecological economics has the explicit goal of sustainable scale (rather than continual growth), <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_distribution" title="Sustainable distribution">fair distribution</a> and efficient allocation, in that order.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">[176]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Costanza_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costanza-177">[177]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/World_Business_Council_for_Sustainable_Development" title="World Business Council for Sustainable Development">World Business Council for Sustainable Development</a> states that "business cannot succeed in societies that fail".<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">[178]</a></sup></p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Economic" class="mw-redirect" title="Economic">economic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_policy" title="Environmental policy">environmental</a> fields, the term <a href="/wiki/Decoupling_(utility_regulation)" title="Decoupling (utility regulation)">decoupling</a> is becoming increasingly used in the context of economic production and environmental quality. When used in this way, it refers to the ability of an economy to grow without incurring corresponding increases in environmental pressure. Ecological economics includes the study of societal metabolism, the throughput of resources that enter and exit the economic system in relation to <a href="/wiki/Environmental_quality" title="Environmental quality">environmental quality</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Costanza_177-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Costanza-177">[177]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup> An economy that is able to sustain GDP growth without having a negative impact on the environment is said to be decoupled. Exactly how, if, or to what extent this can be achieved is a subject of much debate. In 2011 the <a href="/wiki/International_Resource_Panel" title="International Resource Panel">International Resource Panel</a>, hosted by the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Environment_Programme" title="United Nations Environment Programme">United Nations Environment Programme</a> (UNEP), warned that by 2050 the human race could be devouring 140 billion tons of minerals, ores, fossil fuels and biomass per year&#160;– three times its current rate of consumption&#160;– unless nations can make serious attempts at decoupling.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> The report noted that citizens of developed countries consume an average of 16 tons of those four key resources per capita per annum (ranging up to 40 or more tons per person in some developed countries). By comparison, the average person in India today consumes four tons per year. Sustainability studies analyse ways to reduce <a href="/wiki/Resource_intensity" title="Resource intensity">resource intensity</a> (the amount of resource (e.g. water, energy, or materials) needed for the production, consumption and disposal of a unit of good or service) whether this be achieved from improved economic management, product design, or new technology.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>There are conflicting views whether improvements in technological efficiency and innovation will enable a complete decoupling of economic growth from environmental degradation. On the one hand, it has been claimed repeatedly by efficiency experts that resource use intensity (i.e., energy and materials use per unit <a href="/wiki/GDP" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP">GDP</a>) could in principle be reduced by at least four or five-fold, thereby allowing for continued economic growth without increasing resource depletion and associated pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[182]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[183]</a></sup> On the other hand, an extensive historical analysis of technological efficiency improvements has conclusively shown that improvements in the efficiency of the use of energy and materials were almost always outpaced by economic growth, in large part because of the <a href="/wiki/Rebound_effect_(conservation)" title="Rebound effect (conservation)">rebound effect (conservation)</a> or <a href="/wiki/Jevons_Paradox" class="mw-redirect" title="Jevons Paradox">Jevons Paradox</a> resulting in a net increase in resource use and associated pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[184]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[185]</a></sup> Furthermore, there are inherent thermodynamic (i.e., <a href="/wiki/Second_law_of_thermodynamics" title="Second law of thermodynamics">second law of thermodynamics</a>) and practical limits to all efficiency improvements. For example, there are certain minimum unavoidable material requirements for growing food, and there are limits to making automobiles, houses, furniture, and other products lighter and thinner without the risk of losing their necessary functions.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">[186]</a></sup> Since it is both theoretically and practically impossible to increase resource use efficiencies indefinitely, it is equally impossible to have continued and infinite economic growth without a concomitant increase in resource depletion and environmental pollution, i.e., economic growth and resource depletion can be decoupled to some degree over the short run but not the long run. Consequently, long-term sustainability requires the transition to a <a href="/wiki/Steady_state_economy" class="mw-redirect" title="Steady state economy">steady state economy</a> in which total GDP remains more or less constant, as has been advocated for decades by <a href="/wiki/Herman_Daly" title="Herman Daly">Herman Daly</a> and others in the <a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a> community.</p>
<p>A different proposed solution to partially decouple economic growth from environmental degradation is the <i>restore</i> approach.<sup id="cite_ref-restore_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-restore-187">[187]</a></sup> This approach views "restore" as a fourth component to the common reduce, reuse, recycle motto. Participants in such efforts are encouraged to voluntarily donate towards nature conservation a small fraction of the financial savings they experience through a more frugal use of resources. These financial savings would normally lead to rebound effects, but a theoretical analysis suggests that donating even a small fraction of the experienced savings can potentially more than eliminate rebound effects.<sup id="cite_ref-restore_187-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-restore-187">[187]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nature_as_an_economic_externality">Nature as an economic externality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Nature as an economic externality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" class="image"><img alt="Deforastation of native rain forest in Rio de Janeiro City for extraction of clay for civil construction" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/300px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" width="300" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/450px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg/600px-Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2544" data-file-height="1696" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hillside_deforestation_in_Rio_de_Janeiro.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Deforestation" title="Deforestation">Deforestation</a> of native <a href="/wiki/Rain_forest" class="mw-redirect" title="Rain forest">rain forest</a> in Rio de Janeiro City for extraction of <a href="/wiki/Clay" title="Clay">clay</a> for <a href="/wiki/Civil_engineering" title="Civil engineering">civil engineering</a> (2009 picture)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">Ecosystem services</a></div>
<p>The economic importance of nature is indicated by the use of the expression <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem_services" title="Ecosystem services">ecosystem services</a> to highlight the market relevance of an increasingly scarce natural world that can no longer be regarded as both unlimited and free.<sup id="cite_ref-Tragedy_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tragedy-188">[188]</a></sup> In general, as a <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a> or service becomes more scarce the <a href="/wiki/Price" title="Price">price</a> increases and this acts as a restraint that encourages frugality, technical innovation and alternative products. However, this only applies when the product or service falls within the market system.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">[189]</a></sup> As ecosystem services are generally treated as economic <a href="/wiki/Externalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Externalities">externalities</a> they are unpriced and therefore overused and degraded, a situation sometimes referred to as the <a href="/wiki/Tragedy_of_the_Commons" class="mw-redirect" title="Tragedy of the Commons">Tragedy of the Commons</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Tragedy_188-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tragedy-188">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>One approach to this dilemma has been the attempt to "internalize" these "externalities" by using market strategies like <a href="/wiki/Ecotaxes" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecotaxes">ecotaxes</a> and incentives, tradeable permits for carbon, and the encouragement of <a href="/wiki/Payment_for_ecosystem_services" title="Payment for ecosystem services">payment for ecosystem services</a>. <a href="/wiki/Community_currencies" class="mw-redirect" title="Community currencies">Community currencies</a> associated with <a href="/wiki/Local_Exchange_Trading_Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Local Exchange Trading Systems">Local Exchange Trading Systems</a> (LETS), a <a href="/wiki/Gift_economy" title="Gift economy">gift economy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Time_Banking" class="mw-redirect" title="Time Banking">Time Banking</a> have also been promoted as a way of supporting local economies and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">[190]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[191]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Green_economics" class="mw-redirect" title="Green economics">Green economics</a> is another market-based attempt to address issues of equity and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">[192]</a></sup> The global recession and a range of associated government policies are likely to bring the biggest annual fall in the world's carbon dioxide emissions in 40 years.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[193]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_opportunity">Economic opportunity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Economic opportunity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Treating the environment as an externality may generate short-term profit at the expense of sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[194]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_business" title="Sustainable business">Sustainable business</a> practices, on the other hand, integrate ecological concerns with social and economic ones (i.e., the <a href="/wiki/Triple_bottom_line" title="Triple bottom line">triple bottom line</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[195]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[196]</a></sup> Growth that depletes ecosystem services is sometimes termed "<a href="/wiki/Uneconomic_growth" title="Uneconomic growth">uneconomic growth</a>" as it leads to a decline in <a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Minimizing such growth can provide opportunities for local businesses. For example, industrial waste can be treated as an "economic resource in the wrong place". The benefits of <a href="/wiki/Waste_reduction" class="mw-redirect" title="Waste reduction">waste reduction</a> include savings from disposal costs, fewer environmental penalties, and reduced liability insurance. This may lead to increased market share due to an improved public image.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">[199]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">[200]</a></sup> Energy efficiency can also increase profits by reducing costs.</p>
<p>The idea of sustainability as a business opportunity has led to the formation of organizations such as the Sustainability Consortium of the <a href="/wiki/Society_for_Organizational_Learning" title="Society for Organizational Learning">Society for Organizational Learning</a>, the Sustainable Business Institute, and the World Council for Sustainable Development.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[201]</a></sup> The expansion of sustainable business opportunities can contribute to <a href="/wiki/Job_creation" class="mw-redirect" title="Job creation">job creation</a> through the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Green-collar" class="mw-redirect" title="Green-collar">green-collar</a> workers.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup> Research focusing on progressive corporate leaders who have integrated sustainability into commercial strategy has yielded a leadership competency model for sustainability,<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> and led to emergence of the concept of "embedded sustainability" – defined by its authors Chris Laszlo and <a href="/wiki/Nadya_Zhexembayeva" title="Nadya Zhexembayeva">Nadya Zhexembayeva</a> as "incorporation of environmental, health, and social value into the core business with no trade-off in price or quality&#160;– in other words, with no social or green premium."<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">[205]</a></sup> Laszlo and Zhexembayeva's research showed that embedded sustainability offers at least seven distinct opportunities for business value creation: a) better risk-management, b) increased efficiency through reduced waste and resource use, c) better product differentiation, d) new market entrances, e) enhanced brand and reputation, f) greater opportunity to influence industry standards, and g) greater opportunity for radical innovation.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">[206]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nadya_Zhexembayeva" title="Nadya Zhexembayeva">Nadya Zhexembayeva</a>'s 2014 research further suggested that innovation driven by resource depletion can result in fundamental advantages for company products and services, as well as the company strategy as a whole, when right principles of innovation are applied.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">[207]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecosocialist_approach">Ecosocialist approach</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Ecosocialist approach">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Eco-socialism" title="Eco-socialism">Eco-socialism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxism</a></div>
<p>One school of thought, often labeled <a href="/wiki/Eco-socialism" title="Eco-socialism">ecosocialism</a> or ecological Marxism, asserts that the <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalist economic system</a> is fundamentally incompatible with the ecological and social requirements of sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">[208]</a></sup> This theory rests on the premises that:</p>
<ol>
<li>Capitalism’s sole economic purpose is “unlimited <a href="/wiki/Capital_accumulation" title="Capital accumulation">capital accumulation</a>” in the hands of the <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">capitalist class</a><sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">[209]</a></sup></li>
<li>The urge to accumulate (the <a href="/wiki/Profit_motive" title="Profit motive">profit motive</a>) drives capitalists to continually reinvest and expand production, creating indefinite and unsustainable economic growth<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">[210]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">[211]</a></sup></li>
<li>“Capital tends to degrade the conditions of its own production” (the ecosystems and resources on which any economy depends)<sup id="cite_ref-Kovel_2007.2C_p._38_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kovel_2007.2C_p._38-212">[212]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>Thus, according to this analysis:</p>
<ol>
<li>Giving economic priority to the fulfillment of <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human needs</a> while staying within ecological limits, as <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_development" title="Sustainable development">sustainable development</a> demands, is in conflict with the structural workings of capitalism<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">[213]</a></sup></li>
<li>A steady-state capitalist economy is impossible;<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">[214]</a></sup> further, a steady-state capitalist economy is socially undesirable due to the inevitable outcome of massive <a href="/wiki/Unemployment" title="Unemployment">unemployment and underemployment</a><sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">[215]</a></sup></li>
<li>Capitalism will, unless overcome by <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolution</a>, run up against the physical limits of the biosphere and <a href="/wiki/Societal_collapse" title="Societal collapse">self-destruct</a><sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[216]</a></sup></li>
</ol>
<p>By this logic, market-based solutions to ecological crises (<a href="/wiki/Ecological_economics" title="Ecological economics">ecological economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_economics" title="Environmental economics">environmental economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Green_economy" title="Green economy">green economy</a>) are rejected as technical tweaks that do not confront capitalism’s structural failures.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">[217]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> “Low-risk” technology/science-based solutions such as <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_agriculture" title="Sustainable agriculture">sustainable agriculture</a>, and increases in <a href="/wiki/Energy_conversion_efficiency" title="Energy conversion efficiency">energy efficiency</a> are seen as necessary but insufficient.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">[219]</a></sup> “High-risk” technological solutions such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_power" title="Nuclear power">nuclear power</a> and <a href="/wiki/Climate_engineering" title="Climate engineering">climate engineering</a> are entirely rejected.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">[220]</a></sup> Attempts made by businesses to “<a href="/wiki/Greenwashing" title="Greenwashing">greenwash</a>” their practices are regarded as false advertising, and it is pointed out that implementation of renewable technology (such as <a href="/wiki/Walmart" title="Walmart">Walmart</a>’s proposition to supply their electricity with solar power) has <a href="/wiki/Jevons_paradox" title="Jevons paradox">the effect opposite of reductions in resource consumption</a>, viz. further economic growth.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">[221]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_business" title="Sustainable business">Sustainable business</a> models and the <a href="/wiki/Triple_bottom_line" title="Triple bottom line">triple bottom line</a> are viewed as morally praiseworthy but ignorant to the tendency in capitalism for the <a href="/wiki/Distribution_of_wealth" title="Distribution of wealth">distribution of wealth</a> to become increasingly unequal and socially unstable/unsustainable.<sup id="cite_ref-Kovel_2007.2C_p._38_212-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kovel_2007.2C_p._38-212">[212]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">[222]</a></sup> Ecosocialists claim that the general unwillingness of capitalists to tolerate—and capitalist governments to implement—constraints on maximum profit (such as ecotaxes or preservation and conservation measures) renders <a href="/wiki/Environmental_law" title="Environmental law">environmental reforms</a> incapable of facilitating large-scale change: “History teaches us that although capitalism has at times responded to environmental movements . . . at a certain point, at which the system’s underlying accumulation drive is affected, its resistance to environmental demands stiffens.”<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">[223]</a></sup> They also note that, up until the event of total <a href="/wiki/Ecological_collapse" title="Ecological collapse">ecological collapse</a>, destruction caused by natural disasters generally causes an increase in economic growth and accumulation; thus, capitalists have no foreseeable motivation to reduce the probability of disasters (i.e. convert to sustainable/ecological production).<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">[224]</a></sup></p>
<p>Ecosocialists advocate for the <a href="/wiki/Revolution" title="Revolution">revolutionary</a> succession of capitalism by ecosocialism—an <a href="/wiki/Egalitarianism" title="Egalitarianism">egalitarian</a> economic/political/social structure designed to harmonize human society with non-human ecology and to fulfill <a href="/wiki/Human_rights" title="Human rights">human needs</a>—as the only sufficient solution to the present-day ecological crisis, and hence the only path towards sustainability.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">[225]</a></sup> Sustainability is viewed not as a domain exclusive to scientists, environmental activists, and business leaders but as a holistic project that must involve the whole of humanity redefining its place in <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">Nature</a>: “What every environmentalist needs to know . . . is that capitalism is not the solution but the problem, and that if humanity is going to survive this crisis, it will do so because it has exercised its capacity for human freedom, through social struggle, in order to create a whole new world—in coevolution with the planet.”<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">[226]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Social_dimension">Social dimension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Social dimension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Social_sustainability" title="Social sustainability">Social sustainability</a></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_policy" title="Environmental policy">Environmental policy</a></div>
<p>Sustainability issues are generally expressed in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">scientific</a> and environmental terms, as well as in ethical terms of <a href="/wiki/Stewardship" title="Stewardship">stewardship</a>, but implementing change is a social challenge that entails, among other things, <a href="/wiki/International_law" title="International law">international</a> and national <a href="/wiki/Law" title="Law">law</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urban_planning" title="Urban planning">urban planning</a> and transport, local and individual <a href="/wiki/Lifestyle_(sociology)" title="Lifestyle (sociology)">lifestyles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethical_consumerism" title="Ethical consumerism">ethical consumerism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">[227]</a></sup> "The relationship between human rights and human development, corporate power and environmental justice, global poverty and citizen action, suggest that responsible global citizenship is an inescapable element of what may at first glance seem to be simply matters of personal consumer and moral choice."<sup id="cite_ref-Blewitt2008.2C96_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blewitt2008.2C96-228">[228]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Peace.2C_security.2C_social_justice">Peace, security, social justice</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Peace, security, social justice">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Peace" title="Peace">Peace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_justice" title="Social justice">Social justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Environmental_justice" title="Environmental justice">Environmental justice</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_ethics" title="Environmental ethics">Environmental ethics</a></div>
<p>Social disruptions like <a href="/wiki/War" title="War">war</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crime" title="Crime">crime</a> and <a href="/wiki/Political_corruption" title="Political corruption">corruption</a> divert resources from areas of greatest human need, damage the capacity of societies to plan for the future, and generally threaten human well-being and the environment.<sup id="cite_ref-Blewitt2008.2C96_228-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blewitt2008.2C96-228">[228]</a></sup> Broad-based strategies for more sustainable social systems include: improved education and the political empowerment of women, especially in developing countries; greater regard for social justice, notably equity between rich and poor both within and between countries; and intergenerational equity.<sup id="cite_ref-Cohen2006_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen2006-76">[76]</a></sup> Depletion of natural resources including fresh water<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">[229]</a></sup> increases the likelihood of “resource wars”.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">[230]</a></sup> This aspect of sustainability has been referred to as <a href="/wiki/Environmental_security" title="Environmental security">environmental security</a> and creates a clear need for <a href="/wiki/List_of_environmental_agreements" class="mw-redirect" title="List of environmental agreements">global environmental agreements</a> to manage resources such as aquifers and rivers which span political boundaries, and to protect shared global systems including <a href="/wiki/Oceans" class="mw-redirect" title="Oceans">oceans</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">atmosphere</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">[231]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Poverty">Poverty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Poverty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">Poverty</a></div>
<p>A major hurdle to achieve sustainability is the alleviation of poverty. It has been widely acknowledged that poverty is one source of environmental degradation. Such acknowledgment has been made by the <a href="/wiki/Brundtland_Report" class="mw-redirect" title="Brundtland Report">Brundtland Commission report Our Common Future</a><sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup> and the Millennium Development Goals.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">[233]</a></sup> There is a growing realization in national governments and multilateral institutions that it is impossible to separate economic development issues from environment issues: according to the Brundtland report, “poverty is a major cause and effect of global environmental problems. It is therefore futile to attempt to deal with environmental problems without a broader perspective that encompasses the factors underlying world poverty and international inequality.”<sup id="cite_ref-234" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-234">[234]</a></sup> Individuals living in poverty tend to rely heavily on their local ecosystem as a source for basic needs (such as nutrition and medicine) and general well-being.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">[235]</a></sup> As population growth continues to increase, increasing pressure is being placed on the local ecosystem to provide these basic essentials. According to the UN Population Fund, high fertility and poverty have been strongly correlated, and the world’s poorest countries also have the highest fertility and population growth rates.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup> The word sustainability is also used widely by western country development agencies and international charities to focus their poverty alleviation efforts in ways that can be sustained by the local populace and its environment. For example, teaching <a href="/wiki/Water_treatment" title="Water treatment">water treatment</a> to the poor by boiling their water with <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a>, would not generally be considered a sustainable strategy, whereas using <a href="/wiki/Polyethylene_terephthalate" title="Polyethylene terephthalate">PET</a> <a href="/wiki/Solar_water_disinfection" title="Solar water disinfection">solar water disinfection</a> would be. Also, sustainable <a href="/wiki/Best_practices" class="mw-redirect" title="Best practices">best practices</a> can involve the <a href="/wiki/Recycling" title="Recycling">recycling</a> of materials, such as the use of <a href="/wiki/Plastics_recycling" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastics recycling">recycled</a> <a href="/wiki/Plastics" class="mw-redirect" title="Plastics">plastics</a> for lumber where deforestation has devastated a country's timber base. Another example of sustainable practices in poverty alleviation is the use of exported recycled materials from developed to developing countries, such as <a href="/wiki/Bridges_to_Prosperity" title="Bridges to Prosperity">Bridges to Prosperity</a>'s use of wire rope from shipping container <a href="/wiki/Gantry_cranes" class="mw-redirect" title="Gantry cranes">gantry cranes</a> to act as the structural wire rope for <a href="/wiki/Footbridges" class="mw-redirect" title="Footbridges">footbridges</a> that cross rivers in poor rural areas in Asia and Africa.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_relationship_to_nature">Human relationship to nature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Human relationship to nature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Environmental_studies" title="Environmental studies">Environmental studies</a></div>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Murray_Bookchin" title="Murray Bookchin">Murray Bookchin</a>, the idea that humans must dominate nature is common in <a href="/wiki/Social_hierarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="Social hierarchy">hierarchical</a> societies. Bookchin contends that <a href="/wiki/Capitalism" title="Capitalism">capitalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Market_(economics)" title="Market (economics)">market</a> relationships, if unchecked, have the capacity to reduce the planet to a mere resource to be exploited. Nature is thus treated as a <a href="/wiki/Commodity" title="Commodity">commodity</a>: “The plundering of the human spirit by the market place is paralleled by the plundering of the earth by capital.”<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Social_ecology" title="Social ecology">Social ecology</a>, founded by Bookchin, is based on the conviction that nearly all of humanity's present ecological problems originate in, indeed are mere symptoms of, dysfunctional social arrangements. Whereas most authors proceed as if our ecological problems can be fixed by implementing recommendations which stem from physical, biological, economic etc., studies, Bookchin's claim is that these problems can only be resolved by understanding the underlying social processes and intervening in those processes by applying the concepts and methods of the social sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">[238]</a></sup></p>
<p>A pure capitalist approach has also been criticized in <a href="/wiki/Stern_Review_on_the_Economics_of_Climate_Change" class="mw-redirect" title="Stern Review on the Economics of Climate Change">Stern Review on the Economics of Climate Change</a> to <a href="/wiki/Mitigation_of_global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Mitigation of global warming">mitigation</a> the <a href="/wiki/Effects_of_global_warming" title="Effects of global warming">effects of global warming</a> in this excerpt ...</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>“the greatest example of <a href="/wiki/Market_failure" title="Market failure">market failure</a> we have ever seen.”<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p><a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">Deep ecology</a> is a movement founded by <a href="/wiki/Arne_N%C3%A6ss" title="Arne Næss">Arne Naess</a> that establishes principles for the well-being of all life on Earth and the richness and diversity of life forms. The movement advocates, among other things, a substantial decrease in human population and consumption along with the reduction of human interference with the nonhuman world. To achieve this, deep ecologists advocate policies for basic economic, technological, and ideological structures that will improve the <i><a href="/wiki/Quality_of_life" title="Quality of life">quality of life</a></i> rather than the <i><a href="/wiki/Standard_of_living" title="Standard of living">standard of living</a></i>. Those who subscribe to these principles are obliged to make the necessary change happen.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> The concept of a billion-year <a href="/wiki/Sustainocene" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainocene">Sustainocene</a> has been developed to initiate policy consideration of an earth where human structures power and fuel the needs of that species (for example through <a href="/wiki/Artificial_photosynthesis" title="Artificial photosynthesis">artificial photosynthesis</a>) allowing <a href="/wiki/Rights_of_Nature" title="Rights of Nature">Rights of Nature</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">[242]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_settlements">Human settlements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Human settlements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #F9F9F9;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<center><b><a href="/wiki/The_Natural_Step#System_conditions_of_sustainability" title="The Natural Step">Sustainability principles</a></b><br /></center>
<p>1. Reduce dependence upon fossil fuels,<br />
underground metals, and minerals<br />
2. Reduce dependence upon synthetic chemicals<br />
and other unnatural substances<br />
3. Reduce encroachment upon nature<br />
4. Meet human needs fairly &amp; efficiently<sup id="cite_ref-James_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One approach to <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_living" title="Sustainable living">sustainable living</a>, exemplified by small-scale urban <a href="/wiki/Transition_Towns" class="mw-redirect" title="Transition Towns">transition towns</a> and rural <a href="/wiki/Ecovillages" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecovillages">ecovillages</a>, seeks to create self-reliant communities based on principles of <a href="/wiki/Simple_living" title="Simple living">simple living</a>, which maximize <a href="/wiki/Self-sufficiency" title="Self-sufficiency">self-sufficiency</a> particularly in food production. These principles, on a broader scale, underpin the concept of a <a href="/wiki/Bioregionalism" title="Bioregionalism">bioregional</a> economy.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> These approaches often utilize commons based knowledge sharing of <a href="/wiki/Open_source_appropriate_technology" title="Open source appropriate technology">open source appropriate technology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup></p>
<p>Other approaches, loosely based around <a href="/wiki/New_Urbanism" title="New Urbanism">New Urbanism</a>, are successfully reducing environmental impacts by altering the built environment to create and preserve <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_cities" class="mw-redirect" title="Sustainable cities">sustainable cities</a> which support <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_transport" title="Sustainable transport">sustainable transport</a>. Residents in compact urban neighborhoods drive fewer miles, and have significantly lower environmental impacts across a range of measures, compared with those living in <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">sprawling</a> suburbs.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">[246]</a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_architecture" title="Sustainable architecture">sustainable architecture</a> the recent movement of <a href="/wiki/New_Classical_Architecture" class="mw-redirect" title="New Classical Architecture">New Classical Architecture</a> promotes a sustainable approach towards construction, that appreciates and develops <a href="/wiki/Smart_growth" title="Smart growth">smart growth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vernacular_architecture" title="Vernacular architecture">architectural tradition</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical design</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-247" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup> This in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Modern_architecture" title="Modern architecture">modernist</a> and <a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">globally uniform</a> architecture, as well as opposing solitary <a href="/wiki/Housing_estate" title="Housing estate">housing estates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_sprawl" title="Urban sprawl">suburban sprawl</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup> Both trends started in the 1980s. The concept of <a href="/wiki/Circular_flow_land_use_management" title="Circular flow land use management">Circular flow land use management</a> has also been introduced in Europe to promote sustainable land use patterns that strive for compact cities and a reduction of greenfield land take by urban sprawl.</p>
<p>Large scale <a href="/wiki/Social_movements" class="mw-redirect" title="Social movements">social movements</a> can influence both community choices and the built environment. <a href="/wiki/Eco-municipality" title="Eco-municipality">Eco-municipalities</a> may be one such movement.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> Eco-municipalities take a <a href="/wiki/Systems" class="mw-redirect" title="Systems">systems</a> approach, based on sustainability principles. The eco-municipality movement is participatory, involving community members in a bottom-up approach. In Sweden, more than 70 cities and towns—25 per cent of all municipalities in the country—have adopted a common set of <a href="/wiki/The_Natural_Step#System_conditions_of_sustainability" title="The Natural Step">"Sustainability Principles"</a> and implemented these systematically throughout their municipal operations. There are now twelve eco-municipalities in the United States and the <a href="/wiki/American_Planning_Association" title="American Planning Association">American Planning Association</a> has adopted sustainability objectives based on the same principles.<sup id="cite_ref-James_243-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-James-243">[243]</a></sup></p>
<p>There is a wealth of advice available to individuals wishing to reduce their personal and social impact on the environment through small, inexpensive and easily achievable steps.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-252" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-252">[252]</a></sup> But the transition required to reduce global human consumption to within sustainable limits involves much larger changes, at all levels and contexts of society.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253">[253]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> has recognised the central role of education, and have declared a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Decade_of_Education_for_Sustainable_Development" title="United Nations Decade of Education for Sustainable Development">decade of education for sustainable development</a>, 2005–2014, which aims to "challenge us all to adopt new behaviours and practices to secure our future".<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">[254]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Worldwide_Fund_for_Nature" class="mw-redirect" title="Worldwide Fund for Nature">Worldwide Fund for Nature</a> proposes a strategy for sustainability that goes beyond education to tackle underlying individualistic and materialistic <a href="/wiki/Value_(personal_and_cultural)#Cultural_values" class="mw-redirect" title="Value (personal and cultural)">societal values</a> head-on and strengthen people's connections with the natural world.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">[255]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_and_labor_rights">Human and labor rights</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Human and labor rights">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Application of <a href="/wiki/Social_sustainability" title="Social sustainability">social sustainability</a> requires stakeholders to look at human and labor rights, prevention of human trafficking, and other human rights risks.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">[256]</a></sup> These issues should be considered in production and procurement of various worldwide commodities. The international community has identified many industries whose practices have been known to violate social sustainability, and many of these industries have organizations in place that aid in verifying the social sustainability of products and services.<sup id="cite_ref-257" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-257">[257]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Equator_Principles" title="Equator Principles">Equator Principles</a> (financial industry), <a href="/wiki/Fair_Wear_Foundation" title="Fair Wear Foundation">Fair Wear Foundation</a> (garments), and Electronics Industry Citizenship Coalition are examples of such organizations and initiatives. Resources are also available for verifying the life-cycle of products and the producer or vendor level, such as <a href="/wiki/Green_Seal" title="Green Seal">Green Seal</a> for cleaning products, <a href="/wiki/NSF_International" title="NSF International">NSF</a>-140 for carpet production, and even labeling of <a href="/wiki/Organic_food" title="Organic food">Organic food</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">[258]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cultural_dimension">Cultural dimension</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sustainability&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Cultural dimension">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The cultural dimension of sustainability is known as <a href="/w/index.php?title=Culture_21&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Culture 21 (page does not exist)">culture 21</a>, the Agenda 21 for culture and vice versa. At least since 2005, with e.g. the start of UCLG and the first Culture Summit of UCLG in Bilbao - 18 to 20 March 2015, culture is seen as a pillar for Sustainable Development: "The organizing principle for sustainability is sustainable development, which includes the four interconnected domains: ecology, economics, politics and culture.[1]" The model is now being used by organizations such as the United Nations Cities Programme.[40] and Metropolis[41]. Culture 21 is anchored in the <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a>, more particularly in SDG Goal 11. Make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient and sustainable, Chapter 11.4 strengthen efforts to protect and safeguard the world’s cultural and natural heritage". More: SDG Goal 11. Make cities and human settlements inclusive, safe, resilient and sustainable</p>
<p>More: <a rel="nofollow" class="external free" href="http://www.agenda21culture.net/">http://www.agenda21culture.net/</a></p>
